Master Thesis
Globally Differentiated Effect Factors for
Terrestrial Acidification for use in Life Cycle
Impact Assessment
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
Terrestrial acidiﬁcation is one of the impact categories commonly included in
Life Cycle Assessment, a tool that quantiﬁes environmental impacts of the entire
life-cycle of products and services. The emissions and subsequent atmospheric depo-
sition of acidifying substances from human activities cause accelerated acidiﬁcation
of terrestrial ecosystems which aﬀects ecosystem health. In order to quantify the
adverse eﬀects of terrestrial acidiﬁcation, it is necessary to link the acidiﬁcation
of soils to declines in ecosystem quality. This quantiﬁcation is done using eﬀect
factors (EFs), which link a decrease in soil pH to a decline in species richness. The
EFs currently implemented in the LCA methodology lack the appropriate spatial
resolution to properly characterise the eﬀects of terrestrial acidiﬁcation, because
the data underlying the quantiﬁcation of species richness is limited, and EFs are
currently provided at the spatial resolution of the 14 biomes. In this study, a com-
prehensive, global, vascular species inventory covering 49% of all vascular species
is developed and EFs are produced for 688 of the 825 ecoregions of the world.
The EFs show great spatial variability between ecoregions within the same biome,
which emphasises that EFs developed at biome level are not representative and
also prone to underestimate the eﬀects of terrestrial acidiﬁcation. Substantial data
gaps in species occurrence data are identiﬁed, especially in Asia and Africa, and
it is recommended that eﬀorts should be made to better catalogue the biodiver-
sity of these regions.
These results present a substantial step towards spatially
representative and robust EFs, and it is recommended to implement the EFs into
LCA methodology, so that life-cycle impact assessments may properly reﬂect the
diﬀerences in sensitivity of ecosystems towards terrestrial acidiﬁcation.
I
==PAGE:3==
Preface
This thesis is structured as a scientiﬁc article, a format that was decided in collaboration
with my supervisors. The work in this thesis presents signiﬁcant results towards the
assessment of the eﬀects of terrestrial acidiﬁcation with speciﬁc focus on the integration
into Life Cycle Assessment. Because the work is planned to be submitted to the scientiﬁc
journal ”Environmental Science & Technology” (ES&T) it was deemed appropriate to
present the thesis itself as a scientiﬁc article, and the formatting is based on the guide-
lines and templates provided by ES&T, with some modiﬁcations to properly satisfy the
requirements of a master thesis. The thesis is supported by an extensive appendix of
Supporting Information comprising Supporting Figures, Tables, and Methods, which will
be referenced when appropriate throughout the thesis. The work of this master thesis
constitutes 30 ECTS towards a degree in Environmental Engineering. It was conducted
in collaboration with my two supervisors Alexis Laurent and Michael Zwicky Hauschild
from the Quantitative Sustainability Assessment (QSA) research group at the department
of Management Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark.
Annika Lund Gade

==PAGE:5==
1
Introduction
Life cycle assessment (LCA) is a tool that quantiﬁes a broad range of environmental
impacts, such as climate change, eutrophication and acidiﬁcation, that a product or
service is responsible for during its entire life-cycle, from resource extraction to end-of-
life. A life cycle inventory (LCI), which is a list of all material inputs and emissions in a
product’s life cycle, is coupled with the life cycle impact assessment (LCIA) to produce
environmental impact scores. The impact scores can be determined either at midpoint or
endpoint, which refers to the cause-eﬀect chain between emission (or resource extraction)
and adverse eﬀects.
The endpoint indicators in LCA are Human Health, Ecosystem
Quality and Natural Resources. For example, for the most commonly known impact
category, climate change, the impacts associated with an emission of a greenhouse gas
may for example be expressed at midpoint as the ”increase in radiative forcing” and at
endpoint it is expressed as the damage to Human Health and to Ecosystem Quality.1
LCIA uses a collection of multipliers called characterisation factors (CFs), which
translate emissions into environmental impacts for each impact category. It is the product
of four other multipliers; the fate factor (FF) expresses the transport and transformation
of a given emission, e.g. the mass fraction of a substance emitted to air that ends up in
soil, the exposure factor (XF) expresses the exposure level of organisms to the stressor,
e.g. contact or uptake, the eﬀect factor (EF) expresses the observed adverse eﬀects on
the organism, and the damage factor (DF) expresses the severity of eﬀects.1
Terrestrial acidiﬁcation is one of the impact categories, that are included in the LCIA
methodology, which quantiﬁes the adverse eﬀects on plants from a lowering of the soil
pH. Acidiﬁcation of soil is a natural process, however, the activities of mankind can ac-
celerate this process to unsustainable levels. Terrestrial acidiﬁcation is mainly caused by
atmospheric deposition of acidifying substances. The emissions with the highest acidify-
ing potential which are typically included in the LCIA methodology are sulphur oxides,
nitrogen oxides and ammonia.2,3 It is not the reduction in pH that causes adverse eﬀects,
per se. Rather, a reduction in soil pH will cause metal ions from the soil particles to be
released into the soil water. This leads to leaching and resulting deﬁciency of metal ions
1
==PAGE:6==
that are essential to plant growth and root exposure to other metal ions that are toxic.1,4
The present study focuses speciﬁcally on the development of EFs that will be used to
develop CFs at endpoint, which requires a quantiﬁcation of the eﬀect on ecosystem quality
caused by soil acidiﬁcation. The indicator most commonly used for the quantiﬁcation of
ecosystem quality in LCIA methodology is species richness, which is also the indicator
currently used for terrestrial acidiﬁcation, however there is an increasing focus on the
need to also assess loss in functional diversity.5–7 The current state-of-the-art methods in
EF development in terrestrial acidiﬁcation quantiﬁes eﬀects as the loss of vascular plant
species (ﬂowering plants, herbs and shrubs), that both comprise the majority of plant
species and provide a considerable amount of primary production in terrestrial systems.4,8
The soil pH is used as an indicator for soil acidity, since it is correlated with the presence
of soil nutrients and pollutants and it is a good predictor of plant occurrence.4,9,10 In
terrestrial acidiﬁcation the species richness is deﬁned as the number of distinct, vascular
species present at a soil pH.4
The EFs presently integrated in the LCIA methodology (e.g. ReCiPe20162 and Im-
pactWorld+3) were calculated by Roy et al. 8 using a data set comprising 2,409 distinct
vascular plant species developed by Azevedo et al. 4. There are 383,671 accepted species
of vascular plants11 which indicates that the data set underlying the presently used EF
values may not be representative, since the vascular plant species coverage is only 0.6%.
The EFs are currently provided at the spatial resolution of the 14 world biomes,12 and a
better resolution could not be obtained due to the limitations in the geographical cover-
age of the species data. In a later study by Crespo-Mendes et al. 13 EFs for Brazil were
developed using species occurrence data from an open-access botanical database instead
of sourcing the data from literature (as Azevedo et al.), which resulted in a dramatic
increase in species coverage. This enabled increasing the spatial resolution to the terres-
trial ecoregions of the world12 which subdivides the global landmass into 867 ecoregions.
Their results showed large variation between ecoregions beyond the variation displayed at
biome level, which shows that increased spatial resolution enhances the representativeness
of the EFs.
2
==PAGE:7==
The methods applied in the present study are built upon the methods described by
Crespo-Mendes et al. 13, Azevedo et al. 4 and Roy et al. 8, which have been combined and
modiﬁed. The objective of this study is to develop a comprehensive and representative
global, vascular plant species inventory, which can subsequently be used to develop EFs
for terrestrial acidiﬁcation at two spatial resolutions, biomes and ecoregions. Both the
increase in the number of species covered and the increase in spatial resolution propose a
major improvement to the development of EFs, which may bring forward new insight into
the areas of the world that are most sensitive to acidifying pollution and help concentrate
pollution reduction eﬀorts.
2
Methods
2.1
Development of a global terrestrial plant species inventory
The Global Biodiversity Information Facility (GBIF) is a global research network which
catalogues geotagged species occurrence data in an extensive, standardised, open-access,
online database. As of July 2nd 2019 the GBIF database features 1,328,340,318 species
occurrences from 45,304 data sets provided by 1,419 publishing institutions and GBIF
data has been referenced in 3,732 peer-reviewed papers.14 142,271,907 data points of
vascular plant species occurrences were extracted from the GBIF database.15 The present
study uses vascular species as a proxy for terrestrial species as was done in Azevedo
et al. 4 where Crespo-Mendes et al. 13 used a Brazilian habitat database to determine if a
plant species is terrestrial. Only species occurrences recorded since 1980 are included in
the data set due to the assumption that data from previous years is less representative
and contains more errors and uncertainty. Further details on the download process are
available in Section 1.1 in Supporting Methods.
The species occurrence data is extensive, and it is not feasible to load the approxi-
mately 150GB of data into Excel or even high level programming languages such as R or
Python. Therefore the low level AWK text parsing language16 was used for initial data
cleaning. The preprocessing involved removing data with missing geographic coordinates
3
==PAGE:8==
or species names, records where the taxonomic rank was higher than species, and records
where the taxonomic status was not either ”accepted” or ”synonym”. Detailed methods
on the preprocessing using AWK and the exact number of species removed at each step
is available in Section 1.2 in Supporting Methods. AWK was also used to subset the data
into smaller chunks, which could subsequently be handled in R v3.5.3,17 which is a high
level programming environment for statistical computing.
Taxonomic alignment is a process where species names are compared with a reference
taxonomic backbone and all names are updated to the ”accepted name” which ensures
no double counting due to e.g. synonyms or spelling errors. The online Taxonomic Name
Resolution Service was used to perform taxonomic alignment on the species occurrence
data. It is an online tool for computer-assisted standardisation of plant scientiﬁc names
and was developed by the iPlant Collaborative Tree of Life Project.18 Species categorised
as accepted or synonyms were kept, and the synonyms were translated into their re-
spective accepted names. A detailed description of the taxonomic alignment process is
available in Section 1.3 in Supporting Methods.
The ”Terrestrial ecoregions of the world” (TEOW) divides the global landmass into
867 ecoregions that feature distinct assemblages of communities and species, and these
ecoregions are nested within 14 biomes.12 The WWF provides a spatial data set that
deﬁnes the geographic boundaries of 827 of the ecoregions.19 Two of these ecoregions,
”Rocks and ice” and ”Lakes”, are special areas within the landmass which are irrelevant
to the present study, and they have been excluded. The TEOW data set is used for
the spatial diﬀerentiation in this study, and results are presented both at the spatial
resolution of biomes and ecoregions. Geographic Information Systems (GIS) methods in
R v3.5.317 were used in combination with parallel programming on a high performance
computing (HPC) cluster to assign ecoregions and biomes to the species occurrence data,
and a detailed description of the method is available in Section 2 in Supporting Methods.
4
==PAGE:9==
2.2
Development of species richness distributions
In the introduction, species richness was deﬁned as the number of distinct, vascular, plant
species present at a soil pH.4 In order to develop the species richness distributions, data
on the soil pH of the entire landmass with a suﬃcient spatial resolution is needed.
The International Soil Reference Information Centre (ISRIC) released an updated
version of their open-access SoilGrids system in 2017.
It provides global predictions
for standard numeric soil properties including soil pH at 250m resolution at 7 standard
depths (0, 5, 15, 30, 60, 100 and 200 cm) with 0.1 pH unit resolution.20 SoilGrids250m
was developed using Machine Learning on available measurement data, which means
the data set spans the entire landmass except Antarctica. This data set was used to
couple the species occurrence data with soil pH to produce a species richness distribution.
SoilGrids250m is an improved version of the SoilGrids1km data used in Crespo-Mendes
et al. 21 with 4 times better resolution and also increased accuracy due to an improvement
in their methods.
The individual raster layer pH values at depths between 0cm and
60cm, which were downloaded from the Soilgrids250m database,22 were combined using
a weighted average as recommended by Ribeiro et al., where Crespo-Mendes et al. used
an arithmetic average. More information on SoilGrids250m and a detalied desciption
of the calculation of the weighted average [H+] is available in Section 3 in Supporting
Methods.
Using GIS methods in R v3.5.317 in combination with parallel programming on a
HPC cluster, the species occurrence data was overlayed with the SoilGrids250m and
each species occurrence was assigned a pH. Because the soil pH data is generated by a
model, it has no ”gaps”, however, there are millions of grid cells with ”missing data”
e.g. at the locations of lakes and rivers. Many of the occurrences are reported within
SoilGrids grid cells with no pH, which is probably due to the inaccuracy in the GBIF
data’s coordinates. For 86% of these species occurrences, the pH is approximated using
the average of neighbouring cells, and the remaining 14% are dropped from the data. The
species richness distribution is subsequently calculated as the number of distinct species
found at each pH level, where species presence is assumed at all pH units between the
5
==PAGE:10==
minimum and maximum pH where the species has been observed. A detailed description
of the method is available in Section 4 in Supporting Methods.
2.3
Eﬀect factor computation and regression analysis
The EFs that are currently implemented in LCIA methodology are marginal EFs, which
means they represent the marginal change in species richness when there is a marginal
decrease in pH away from the pH optimum. This means that only the ”acid side” (where
pH is less than the optimum pH) is included in the model, and the ”alkaline side” (where
pH is greater than the optimum pH) is disregarded. The species richness curve then
resembles a dose-response curve, which can be modelled using a logistic function.4,8 This
is also the method that is applied in the present study, and a brief summary of the
calculation approach is presented below. Other approaches do exist, for example, instead
of the marginal approach, one may use the linear or the average approach, but Roy
et al. 8 showed that this choice does not signiﬁcantly change the resulting EF values, and
the marginal approach is applied here in alignment with previous studies. Also, Crespo-
Mendes et al. 13 used a lognormal function to describe the full pH range instead of just the
acidic side. In such an approach, it is possible to obtain negative EFs, where a decrease
in pH towards the pH optimum leads to an increase in species richness. This approach
was disregarded in the present study because the potential increase in species richness
is not necessarily associated with an increase in ecosystem quality. The functions of the
ecosystem and its biodiversity may indeed be signiﬁcantly altered despite an increase in
species richness.23 Without indications and integration of species functionality to resolve
this aspect, a sole focus on the acidic side of the species richness curve was preferred.
Before performing the regression, the species richness distribution is translated into
the estimated Potentially Not Occurring Fraction (PNOF) of species, which ranges be-
tween 0 (at the pH optimum) and 1 (where the species richness is zero):
PNOFi,j = 1 −
SRi,j
SRmax,j
(1)
6
==PAGE:11==
where SRi,j is the species richness at pH i and biome/ecoregion j and SRmax,j is the
maximum species richness for biome/ecoregion j.24 The marginal EF is deﬁned as
EFi,j = dPNOFi,j
dC
(2)
where C is the H+ concentration, which will be referred to as [H+].8,25
A logistic function is ﬁtted to the estimated PNOF using non-linear regression
PNOFi,j =
1
1 + exp log10 (C)+α
β

and the EF when using the logistic function deﬁned in eq. 3 is
EFi,j = −PNOF 2

where α and β are ﬁtting parameters.
Eq.
4 estimates EFs for each pH unit within the pH range included in the data
set, however the EF value at biome/ecoregion level is a single number. Two competing
methods exist for the calculation of an overall EF for a biome/ecoregion. The approach
which is currently implemented in LCIA methodology uses the EF calculated for the pH
where PNOF = 0.5 (EFPNOF=0.5), which is analogous with methods applied in ecotoxi-
cology.4,8,26 A limitation to this approach is that most of the information contained in the
species richness distribution is not utilised, when only the characteristic of the curve at
one pH value is propagated to the reported EF. It is indeed a possibility that the EF value
at this speciﬁc pH value does not represent the biome or ecoregion all that well. Also,
Roy et al. 8 found that there was high uncertainty on the EF value estimates tied to this
approach. Crespo-Mendes et al. 13 proposed developing an area weighted EF (EFAW),
where the EF at each pH unit is weighted by the area which that pH unit covers within
that biome/ecoregion:
EFAW,j =
P
i(Ai,j · EFi,j)
Atot,j
(5)
7
==PAGE:12==
This approach integrates that a larger area has a higher probability of receiving acidifying
substances i.e. the most common pH units have a higher probability of changing and this
is estimated to be a more robust approach than EFPNOF=0.5. To enable comparison with
previous studies, both approaches are applied in the present study.
For nonlinear regression it is not trivial to evaluate how well a ﬁt represents data. It
has therefore been imperative to the present study to develop a substantial and structured
evaluation procedure for the non-linear ﬁtting of the logistic function to the PNOF curves.
Several statistics were tested and the generalised adjusted R2 as deﬁned by Alexander
et al. 27 which is valid for nonlinear regression was selected as the best goodnes-of-ﬁt
indicator which is reported for all ﬁts at both biome and ecoregion level. A detailed
description of the 8 ﬁt statistics that were applied is available in Section 5 in Supporting
Methods.
For linear regression the 95% conﬁdence interval (CI) on predicted values can be
estimated as the predicted values calculated using the upper and lower bounds on the ﬁt
parameters. For nonlinear regression it is less trivial, because using e.g. the upper bounds
on the ﬁt parameter estimates don’t necessarily result in a higher predicted value than for
the estimated value, due to the nonlinearity. Also, it is not the CI of the prediction value
(PNOF) that is being calculated here, but the 95% CI of the EF value, the derivative
of the PNOF curve with respect to [H+]. The CI of the EF value is calculated as the
highest and lowest EFs produced using all possible permutations of the 95% conﬁdence
bounds of the ﬁt parameters (low α with low β, low α with high β, high α with low β
and high α with high β).
Because it is not possible to provide EFs for all ecoregions due to lack of data and
variation in the goodness-of-ﬁt, a set of recommended EFs is developed. For ecoregions
where the EF at ecoregion level cannot be used, it is recommended to use the EF at
biome level instead. When calculating the 95% conﬁdence interval of the ﬁt parameters
(α and β), the upper bound on β (the slope) may become positive. Since the slope of
the acid side curve is inherently negative, this upper bound is invalid. For the parameter
estimates where this was the case, the upper bound was replaced by the highest upper
8
==PAGE:13==
bound on the estimate of β of the ecoregions within the same biome, which is indicated
in the reported ﬁtting parameters. For some ecoregions where the upper bound on the
β parameter is replaced, the upper bound on the EF is lower than the estimated value,
which is meaningless. The upper bound is instead replaced by the highest upper bound of
the EF values of the ecoregions within the same biome, which is indicated in the reported
EFs.
In order to determine if a ﬁt for an ecoregion is good enough to be used as recom-
mended EFs, the ﬁts were divided into 3 groups based on the R2
adj. For ecoregions where
the ﬁt has R2
adj ≤0.6 the recommended EFs are based on the relevant biome EF. For
ecoregions where the ﬁt has 0.6 < R2
adj ≤0.8 the recommended EFs are based on ecore-
gions, but the value is ﬂagged to indicate the higher uncertainty of the estimate. For
ecoregions where the ﬁt has 0.8 < R2
adj the EF can be recommended without reserve.
2.4
Uncertainties and limitations
There are some uncertainties associated with the species occurrence data used to develop
species richness distributions. First of all the spatial coordinates are not very precise. The
species occurrences downloaded from GBIF are sometimes associated with a quantiﬁed
precision, which may be many hundreds of meters, but most occurrences do not have
this. It was not feasible to only include data with quantiﬁed precision since this would
reduce the data set substantially, but the strength of the GBIF data is not its precision
but the abundance, and it is estimated that the imprecision is negligible if the number
of species occurrences recorded within an ecoregion is large enough.
It is an underlying assumption of the present study that the species included in the
data are aﬀected by a change in the soil pH. However, the species occurrence data includes
all vascular plants, which also includes plants that grow on rocks, on other plants or
even are primarily growing in water. GBIF provides a ”habitat” feature in the species
occurrence data, but this feature is only supplied for a very small fraction of the data
and is not suﬃciently diﬀerentiated for this purpose since the categories supplied are
”terrestrial”, ”aquatic” and ”marine”. A recently published growth-form database of
9
==PAGE:14==
143,616 vascular plant species could be applied to test the sensitivity of the calculated
EFs to the inclusion of plants not growing directly in soil.28 However, their analysis
reveals that 72% of the vascular species in their data set are free-standing. Also, plants
growing on other plants may be indirectly aﬀected by soil acidiﬁcation if their host is.
Therefore the integration of the growth-form database is estimated to have little eﬀect
on the EF calculations, but further studies should explore the inﬂuence of such reﬁning
of the dataset on the results.
Substantial uncertainty is associated with the modelling of the PNOF curves. The
uncertainty is quantiﬁed in the conﬁdence intervals and propagated to the EF values.
Additional uncertainty is introduced in the use of proxies for the CIs for some ecoregions,
but these uncertainties are transparently documented. Subsequently, uncertainty is asso-
ciated with the choice of using either a PNOF= 0.5 or area weighted approach. For the
PNOF approach, the EF is very sensitive to the slightest variation in the estimate of the
slope. For the area weighted approach, there is a diﬀerent more complex issue; PNOF
is deﬁned as a range between 0 and 1, however, because the logistic function is ﬁtted to
the PNOF that is derived from observation data, the predicted values at the pH limits
(min pH and pH optimum) do not necessarily reach values of PNOF= 1 and PNOF= 0,
respectively. This has negligible eﬀect on the PNOF= 0.5 based EF, but may have an
eﬀect when performing area weighting. At the pH optimum the EF is supposed to be
0, but may have a ﬁnite value, which could distort the value of EFAW. This issue could
be handled by ﬁtting the curve to the observation data and calculating the PNOF based
on the predicted values from the ﬁtted curve, as was done in Crespo-Mendes et al. 21.
However, this has other drawbacks e.g. the optimum pH and maximum species richness
may no longer align with observed data as was the case in Crespo-Mendes et al. 21. Ei-
ther approach introduces discrepancies in the calculation of area weigted EFs. The latter
better describes the theoretical deﬁnition of the PNOF, but the ﬁrst approach is applied
in the present study, because it preserves the characteristics of the observation data.
Finally there is uncertainty associated with the many modelling choices made through-
out this study. Notably the choice to model vascular species rather than all plant species,
10
==PAGE:15==
the choice to model marginal EFs rather than linear or average EFs, the choice of rela-
tive species richness as the indicator of ecosystem quality etc. However, these choices are
necessary to make the study and the quantiﬁcation of terrestrial acidiﬁcation feasible,
yet they should be kept in mind when interpreting the results.
3
Results and discussion
3.1
Comprehensive mapping of plant species and species rich-
ness distributions
Detailed information on the data collected and used in the development of the species
inventory and species richness distributions at biome level are presented in Table 1. A
data set of 113,846,141 species occurrences was used in the development of EFs. This is
lower than the 142,272,730 species occurrences initially collected from GBIF. Removal of
data points was mainly due to the missing coordinates or species names, no taxonomic
match, or data located outside the terrestrial ecoregions.
A total number of 189,185
distinct species are included in this study, which covers 49% of the 383,671 accepted
plant species.
The total area covered by the 825 ecoregions included in the present study covers
135,204,058 km2 out of the approximately 148,940,000 km2 of total land area,29 which
is 91%. 8% of the total land unaccounted for comprises lakes, rocks and ice, and the
remaining 1% is assumed to comprise the 40 ecoregions not included in the WWF TEOW
data set out of the 867 ecoregions deﬁned in Olson et al. 12, and also attributed the
uncertainty in the area calculations

At the biome level, the numbers in Table 1 reveals both variety in data availability
and characteristics of the biomes. It should be noted that looking directly at the number
of distinct species in a biome may be misleading and should be complemented with
considering the number of distinct species per area. In this study Tropical and Subtropical
Moist Broadleaf Forests has the most distinct species (107,148). However, it is only the
eighth most species rich biome per area unit (54 species/10,000km2). The deﬁnitively
most species rich biomes per area are Mangroves (367 species/10,000km2) and Tropical
and Subtropical Coniferous Forests (274 species/10,000km2). Mangroves only covers a
relatively small land area and there are relatively few species occurrence data points
within this biome, but it still has signiﬁcantly more distinct species per area than any
other biome.
The Tundra appears to be a very species-poor biome, which does not
12
==PAGE:17==
seem to be related to data scarcity as there are more than 1 million species occurrences
recorded within this biome. The above ﬁndings, except the low species richness per area
for Tropical and Subtropical Moist Broadleaf Forests, are in accordance with studies that
have investigated the global plant species richness patterns.30,31
Finally the pH values where species occurrences are recorded range between 3.5 and
9.8 with some variation within biomes, however the variation in optimum pH is somewhat
more notable with most biomes having an optimum pH between 5 and 6 with the excep-
tions of Temperate Grasslands, Savannas and Shrublands (6.5), Mediterranean Forests,
Woodlands and Scrub (6.7) and Deserts and Xeric Shrublands (7.1). These results are
quite diﬀerent from the ones reported by Azevedo et al. 4 where the pH range of species
occurrence was 2.4-10.5 and the range of pH optimum was 4.1-7.8, where both ranges
are much broader than what is found in the present study. This diﬀerence is thought
to be related to the diﬀerence in how the species occurrence data was collected. Since
the data collected in Azevedo et al. 4 is based on peer reviewed studies that link species
occurrences with direct measurements of soil pH, the pH ranges found in their study may
be more representative of actual conditions.
Species richness distributions are presented for all biomes in Figure 1. As a function of
pH the data appears smooth and bell shaped indicating there is a unique pH optimum for
each biome. Exceptions to this smoothness are found for Deserts and Xeric Shrublands
and Mangroves that both have a shoulder on the species richness distribution plot, which
could be due to uncertainty in the data or a characteristic of the biome e.g. underlying
ecoregions having very diﬀerent optimum pH. A comparison with the characteristics of
the plant species inventory at ecoregion, which is presented in Table S7 in Supporting
Tables, shows that the latter is indeed the case. Both biomes feature one or two ecoregions
with a very high number of distinct species at the optimum pH, where this optimum pH
is very diﬀerent from the optimum pH of the biome. All biomes have long tails at one or
both ends of the pH ranges indicating a subset of highly specialised species that manage
to exist at extreme pH conditions, which is in agreement with other studies.9,32

==PAGE:19==
3.2
Eﬀect factors at biome level and inﬂuence of data represen-
tativeness
Table 2 shows the EFs calculated at biome level and opposed to the values derived from
the regression parameters provided in Azevedo et al. 4. The area weighted EFs range
between 1.42E4 PNOF/(mol H+/L) for Boreal Forests and Taiga and 6.29E5 PNOF/(mol
H+/L) for Deserts and Xeric Shrubland. For the PNOF= 0.5 approach the EFs range
between 1.90E4 PNOF/(mol H+/L) for Tropical and Subtropical Moist Broadleaf Forests
and 2.45E5 PNOF/(mol H+/L) for Temperate Grasslands, Savannas and Shrublands.
For 11 out of 14 biomes the EFs are higher for EFPNOF=0.5 than for the area weighted
EFs. The ranking between the biomes change somewhat between the two approaches,
but only marginally, meaning a biome with a high EF value will be one of the highest
ranked biomes no matter which approach is used. The EFs span about 1.5 orders of
magnitude for both approaches. When comparing with the results of Azevedo et al., it
can be observed that the EFs of the present study diﬀer substantially for all biomes by up
to almost two orders of magnitude (largest diﬀerence found for Montane Grasslands and
Shrublands). Also there is less variability between the EFs developed in the present study
(1.5 orders of magnitude instead of 3). The EF value decreases for 3 biomes (Montane
Grasslands and Shrublands, Mediterranean Forests, Woodlands and Scrub and Deserts
and Xeric Shrublands) and increases for the remaining biomes. This also results in a
diﬀerent ranking of the sensitivity of biomes, where e.g. the highest EF is reported for
Temperate Grasslands, Savannas and Shrublands instead of Montane Grasslands and
Shrubland (which has medium sensitivity in the present study). The above changes can
only be explained by the diﬀerence in species occurrence data used, since the applied
method is the same. Fx for Montane Grasslands and Shrublands there is a factor 213
increase in species occurrence data underlying the EF calculation from 138 to 29,387
distinct species when compared with Azevedo et al.. In general, the vascular species
coverage is massively increased from 0.6% in Azevedo et al. to 49% in this study. This
discussion demonstrates the importance of data completeness to arrive at representative
eﬀect characterisation in LCIA.

==PAGE:21==
A comparison with EF values developed in Crespo-Mendes et al. 13 is available in
Table S2 in Supporting Tables. Here the diﬀerence between EFs developed in the two
studies is much less substantial with ratios ranging between 0.66 and 3.64 even though
there is an increase in species occurrence data between a factor 2.7 and 6.1.
There
is one notable exception, Deserts and Xeric Shrublands, where the area weighted EF
changes by almost a factor 14. Diﬀerences in EFs may be explained by the increased
scope, which incorporates areas outside Brazil into the development of EFs for biomes
that also include Brazilian territories.
This is a strong argument that there may be
signiﬁcant diﬀerences in the sensitivity towards acidiﬁcation of ecoregions within a biome.
The diﬀerences can also be associated with the increase in number of data points and
species coverage, or the diﬀerent method used for selecting terrestrial species, however
these aspects are estimated to have negligible inﬂuence on the results in comparison
with the spatial variability between ecoregions.
It can be concluded that developing
global EFs based on regionally comprehensive, yet globally incomplete, species occurrence
data may cause misleading results if applied in regions that exceed their geographic
representativeness.
The robustness of the EF calculation is investigated based on the quality of the ﬁts.
Figure 2 shows the logistic ﬁt to the PNOF curve at biome level represented by a red
line, and the estimated ﬁt parameters are presented in Table S1 in Supporting Tables.
The results of statistical goodness-of-ﬁt testing for each biome are available in Table S3
in Supporting Tables. In summary, the ﬁts show R2
adj values between 0.94 and 1 and
are evaluated to be good at representing the PNOF. Two statistical tests need to be
described further here. The runs-test determines if the function used for a ﬁt leads to
systematic deviations from the underlying data, which indicates whether the function
used is potentially wrong. The D’Agostino-Pearson test evaluates if the residuals are
normally distributed - an underlying assumption of non-linear ﬁtting.
The results of
these two tests for the logistic ﬁt are either mediocre or even poor, which indicates that
the logistic ﬁt is not a perfect representation of the underlying data. There is, however,
no obvious candidate for an alternative function. Since the goodness-of-ﬁt statistics are
17
==PAGE:22==
quite good, it is determined that the ﬁt is suﬃciently good at predicting PNOF-values
for use in the calculation of eﬀect factors.

3.3
Alternative approaches for marginal eﬀect factor modelling
Table 2 shows the EFs calculated at biome level in the present study, which have been
developed using the two diﬀerent approaches to estimating the overall EF for a biome as
described in section 2.3, which are EFPNOF=0.5 and the area weighted EF. The choice of
using the marginal EF at PNOF = 0.5 is questionable for acidiﬁcation, since there is no
argument to favour one point on the curve over another and the soil pH at that point may
cover only a small fraction of the total area that is being characterised. When using the
18
==PAGE:23==
area as a weighting factor, the EF at each pH unit becomes more important if this pH is
prevalent within the biome/ecoregion. The result is a weighted average of the marginals,
which is not dependent on a speciﬁc location on the curve. This approach is analogous
with the development of the long-term marginal electricity mix used in consequential
LCA modelling, where the change in energy production from marginal technologies in-
duced by a small change in the energy demand is weighted by the production capacity
of each technology.33 The EFAW can actually be interpreted as a weighted average of the
marginals, and therefore we recommend this approach in the determination of EF values.
It can be observed in Table 2 that the area weighting generally leads to a slight increase
in EF values compared to the PNOF= 0.5 approach. The ratios of EFAW/EFPNOF=0.5 are
between 2.9 (Deserts and Xeric Shrubland) and 0.5 (Mangoves) and Temperate Conifer
Forests shows the least change. For three biomes the EFAW is lower than EFPNOF=0.5
(Temperate Broadleaf and Mixed Forests, Mediterranean Forests, Woodlands and Scrubs,
Deserts and Xeric Shrublands). This leads to a change in rank of EFs at biome level,
depending on which approach is used. Figure 3 shows a graph of EFPNOF=0.5 vs EFAW
for all biomes and also all ecoregions where an EF has been calculated. There is a clear
pattern that the EFAW tends to be lower than EFPNOF=0.5 and the scatter of the points
shows that there can be a substantial diﬀerence in the EF value calculated using the two
diﬀerent approaches.
Besides the two approaches discussed above, Crespo-Mendes et al. 13 proposed to
develop EFs based on the full pH range of the species distribution, modelled using a
lognormal function. There are two reasons this approach was disregarded in the present
study; for comparison, it was attempted to ﬁt a lognormal function to the full species
richness distribution, but as is shown in Figure 2, the ﬁts are simply not good, a conclusion
that is supported by the complete set of ﬁt statistics provided in Table S4 in Supporting
Tables.
More importantly, even if a decrease in pH results in an increase in species
richness, there may be a loss of species functional diversity, as was described in the
methods. Since functional diversity is not considered in the present study, it is deemed
unwise to provide EFs that quantify a positive eﬀect of terrestrial acidiﬁcation as long as
19
==PAGE:24==
Figure 3: A comparison of EFPNOF=0.5 and EFAW with a black line indicating where
EFPNOF=0.5 = EFAW. The EFs at biome level are emphasised.
20
==PAGE:25==
the EF cannot be complemented by an assessment of functional diversity. The method
used for the lognormal ﬁt is described in Section 6 in Supporting Methods.
3.4
Eﬀect factors at ecoregion level and data completeness
Out of the 825 ecoregions in the TEOW dataset, there are 807 ecoregions with species
occurrence data. For 38 of the 807 ecoregions there were only two or three data points on
the acidic side of the species richness distribution; in those cases a ﬁt was not attempted.
In addition, a ﬁt could not be obtained for 5 other ecoregions. For all these 43 ecoregions,
EF values from their respective biomes are recommended for use as proxies. For the
remaining 764 ecoregions a ﬁt was obtained, and the ﬁt parameters are reported in Table
S5 in Supporting Tables. For 203 of the ﬁtted ecoregions the upper bound on β was
invalid. It was instead replaced by the highest upper bound on the estimate of β of the
ecoregions within the same biome, and the parameter is transparently ﬂagged in Table
S5. The ﬁts are presented in Figure S2 in Supporting Figures.
EFs were determined for the 764 ecoregions with obtained ﬁts and are presented in
Table S6 in Supporting Tables. However, not all these EF values can be directly used. A
set of recommended area weighted EFs were developed, based on the R2
adj as described
in the methods, and are reported separately in Table S6. 688 EFs developed at ecoregion
level are provided as recommended EFs, and the remaining 76 are replaced by the EF
value at biome level as proxy. For 72 of the 688 EFs developed at ecoregion level, the
upper bound on the EF could not be calculated, and the upper bound of the EF at biome
level was used as proxy. All EFs that have been adjusted in any way are transparently
ﬂagged in Table S6.
The 688 recommended (area weighted) EFs at ecoregion level are presented as a map
in Figure 4. Four ecoregions stand out with EFs above 108 PNOF/(mol H+/L); North
Saharan steppe and woodlands, Qaidam Basin semi-desert, Sahara desert and the Gobi
Lakes Valley desert steppe. The ﬁrst three are located in Northern Africa, the latter at
the border between China and Mongolia. In general, ecoregions from the biome Deserts
and Xeric Shrubland dominate the high EFs, which is in agreement with the results
21
==PAGE:26==
of Roy et al. 8, and the 9 highest EFs are from this biome. These very high EFs for
especially deserts in Africa and Asia are probably the explanation for the large increase
in the EF value that was noted for this biome in the comparison with the results from
Crespo-Mendes et al. 13. There are plenty of representatives from other biomes among
the highest EF values though, e.g. there are 4 biomes represented in the top 20 and 9 in
the top 100, which shows that there is a lot of variability that is not represented in EFs at
biome level. The lowest EFs are generally found for ecoregions within the biome Tropical
and Subtropical Moist Broadleaf Forests. Here it should be noted that the EFs describe a
relative change in species richness, not the absolute. The absolute loss of species richness
is also a relevant indicator, but not what is being evaluated here, and the results should
be interpreted with this in mind.
Figure 4: Area weighted eﬀect factors at ecoregion level (only recommended EFs devel-
oped at ecoregion level are shown). Note that the colour scale intervals are progressive.
EFs developed at biome level that are used as proxies are not shown. The upper bound
reﬂects the maximum value in the data
22
==PAGE:27==
3.5
Spatial variability of eﬀect factors and implications for their
use in LCIA
Figure 5 shows the variation of area weighted EF values between ecoregions within the
same biome, which span between 1.5 and 3 orders of magnitude. For all biomes, the EF
values at ecoregion level range between 1.7E3 PNOF/(mol H+/L) and 1.7E8 PNOF/(mol
H+/L). In comparison, the area weighted EF values developed at biome level ranged
between 1.42E4 PNOF/(mol H+/L) and 6.29E5 PNOF/(mol H+/L) and EFs developed
by Azevedo et al. ranged between 8.3E2 and 9.0E6. Figure 5 also shows that an EF
developed at biome level tends to fall in the lower range of EFs developed at ecoregion
level for that biome, which indicates that there is a substantial risk of underestimating
the eﬀect of terrestrial acidiﬁcation in the most vulnerable areas. Due to this general
trend it may be worth considering using the maximum EF within a biome as a proxy
instead of the EF developed at biome level. Roy et al. 8 determined that the EF values
have little inﬂuence on the spatial variability of characterisation factors, however, due to
the increase in spatial variability in the EFs developed at ecoregion level, this may no
longer be the case, and the inﬂuence of the EFs at ecoregion level on the spatial variability
of CFs should be investigated further.

Figure 5: Comparison of the variation between the 688 recommended EFs at ecoregion
level and the corresponding EF at biome level. Note that the y-axis is logarithmic.
A detailed overview of relevant characteristics of the vascular plant species inventory
developed at ecoregion level is presented in Table S7 in Supporting Tables. A graphical
overview of the number of occurrence data points within each ecoregion is presented in
Figure 6. The map highlights that there is a very distinct diﬀerence in the amount of
botanical data available, and regions outside of Europe are undersampled, which is a
well-known fact.34 For most of Asia, certain parts of North and South-West Africa and
the southern part of South America the data is scarce or non-existent, and it should be
recalled that for 18 out of the 825 ecoregions there was no species occurrence data in
the data set at all. There is a clear need for additional investigations into the species
diversity of these areas.
Figure 7 shows the number of distinct species per ecoregion per 10,000 km2. The
highest species richness is reported on tropical islands (top three are Lord Howe Island,
Norfolk Island and Cocos Island) but also forests and montane ecoregions within the
Tropical and Subtropical Moist Broadleaf Forests biome, which all ecoregions with a
24
==PAGE:29==
species richness higher than 10,000 per 10,000 km2 belong to. These extremely species-
rich ecoregions are eﬀectively invisible on the map, because they cover such small areas.
The general patterns of species richness agrees very well with previous studies.30,31,34 The
areas of low species richness in the map in Figure 7 seem highly correlated with the areas
of species occurrence data scarcity in Figure 6. This data scarcity is the main reason that
it is not possible to derive EFs for all ecoregions. In order to properly characterise the
eﬀects of terrestrial acidiﬁcation more data must be gathered for these regions, especially
since the areas of data scarcity also overlap greatly with the biome Deserts and Xeric
Shrubland, which is highly sensitive to acidiﬁcation.
25
==PAGE:30==
Figure 6: Map of the number of recorded vascular species occurrences per ecoregion in
the data set used to develop the species richness distributions. Note that the colour scale
intervals are progressive. The upper bound reﬂects the maximum value in the data.
Figure 7: Map of the number of distinct species per ecoregion per 10,000 km2. Note that
the colour scale intervals are progressive. The upper bound reﬂects the maximum value
in the data.
26
==PAGE:31==
4
Conclusions and recommendations
It was the aim of the present study to develop eﬀect factors for terrestrial acidiﬁcation
which are more spatially representative that the EFs currently implemented in LCIA
methodology. The comprehensive species inventory that was developed enabled the cal-
culation of recommended area weighted EF values at ecoregion level for 688 out of the 825
ecoregions. For the remaining ecoregions it is recommended to use EF values developed
at biome level as a proxy. There is a signiﬁcant variation of EF values between ecoregions
within biomes, and there is a high risk of underestimating the eﬀects of terrestrial acid-
iﬁcation when the spatial resolution of EFs is low. Also, the importance of a complete
species inventory should be stressed, since the species coverage greatly aﬀects the EF
values and a complete data set ensures robust EF values.
In the process of developing the EFs a comprehensive, global, vascular plant species
inventory was developed, which conﬁrmed the current view of where the most species-rich
regions are located in the world. However, substantial data gaps in species occurrence
data was identiﬁed, and it is recommended that eﬀorts should be made to better catalogue
the biodiversity of these regions. Nevertheless, the development of this species inventory
lead to a major improvement in species coverage in EF development from 0.6% to 49%
of all vascular plant species.
The species inventory is potentially very relevant beyond the scope of terrestrial acid-
iﬁcation and even LCA. It is very feasible to utilise the species inventory developed here
for other LCIA impact categories such as eutrophication or land use.
But the data
also presents a global overview of the spatial distribution of species occurrence data and
species richness within biomes, which could have potential relevance beyond LCA e.g. in
ecology.
In further studies, it is recommended to isolate endemic species in the vascular plant
species inventory and develop complementary EFs based on this special group of species,
as was done by Crespo-Mendes et al. 13 for Brazil. It may be that Monte Carlo simulation
or bootstrapping can be used to develop conﬁdence intervals on the EF values without
the use of proxies, which may decrease the model uncertainty of the EF values.

==PAGE:169==
Supporting methods
1
Species inventory
1.1
Download of species occurrence data from the GBIF online database
The species occurrence data was retrieved from the Global Biodiversity Information Facility (GBIF) online
database 12 February 2019. The download is available through the following link: https://doi.org/10.
15468/dl.drtvin. Be aware that the unzipped ﬁle takes up approximately 150GB of memory and cannot
be opened in standard software such as Microsoft Excel. Knowledge of R or Python programming is necessary
to process the data.
The online GBIF occurrence search offers various ﬁlters that are useful for specifying the data that should
be downloaded. The following ﬁlters were used:
• Scientiﬁc Name : Tracheophyta
• Year : 1980-2019
• Basis of record : Exclude “Fossil specimen”
The scientiﬁc name effectively ﬁlters out all other species than those belonging to the phylum Tracheophyta
(vascular species) within the kingdom Plantae. On the day of extraction, there were 238,392,515 occurrences
of tracheophytes out of 252,840,250 occurrences of plants listed in the database. This implies that tracheophytes
are the most reported plant phylum by far.
The GBIF database have records dating centuries back. However, much of that data is based on literature
and sparse. The period 1980-present was chosen on the assumption that data from previous years is less
representative and contains more errors and uncertainty. This choice reduces the occurrences to 142,644,212.
It was decided that occurrences recorded as fossils were not relevant to the study. This reduces the number
of occurrences to 142,271,907.
The download is available in two ﬁle formats; csv ﬁles and the Darwin Core Archive (DaCA) format. For
this study the DaCA format is used because it includes more features of the data than the csv ﬁle download.
There is a discrepancy between the number of occurrences that GBIF reports in the download summary and the
number of occurrences actually in the data ﬁle. GBIF claims the download contains 142,271,907 occurrences,
but there are 142,272,730 occurrences in the download ﬁle.
1.2
Extracting relevant data from the DaCA ﬁle and initial ﬁltering
For the present study only a subset of the data provided in the DaCA ﬁle is used. Within the downloaded folder
there are several ﬁles. The one named occurrence.txt contains the data needed. This ﬁle includes 237 columns
of information (features) for each occurrence record. The size of the ﬁle is reduced to a much more manageable
size by cutting away the superﬂuous features and keeping only the ones relevant to the further analysis. The size
S134
==PAGE:170==
of the data makes it difﬁcult to process the data even with (high level) programming languages since the data
does not ﬁt in RAM. In this study the initial processing was performed using the low level AWK programming
language, which is designed for text processing. AWK is run in the console and is currently installed by default
on all linux distributions. In the present study the AWK processing was performed in Ubuntu 18.04.2 LTS.
The relevant features are decimal longitude, decimal latitude and species name (without author). The taxon
rank and taxonomic status are also extracted temporarily for additional ﬁltering of the data and subsequently
dropped before further analysis. Initially, also the feature "acceptedScientiﬁcNames" was extracted, to inves-
tigate if this feature was taxonomically aligned. If so, it would be possible to bypass taxonomic alignment
via the online Taxonomic Name Resolution Service (TNRS, see Section 1.3). Unfortunately the GBIF feature
"acceptedScientiﬁcNames" is not properly aligned with the results of the TNRS, and it was deemed best to use
the TNRS, which is optimised for this task speciﬁcally and where the selection criteria/taxonomic alignment
steps are more transparent. It has not been possible to ﬁnd any information from GBIF which explains how the
feature "acceptedScientiﬁcNames" is produced.
After reducing the features in the data, AWK is used for initial preprocessing. Species occurrence data
where the species name is missing cannot be used in the analysis and is dropped from the data. 5,185,337
occurrences are dropped on this account. Occurrence data without coordinates is of course irrelevant, because
the data cannot be associated with a location and a soil pH. 8,048,977 occurrences are dropped on this account.
Some occurrence data has the taxon rank "GENUS" which is above species level and therefore irrelevant to the
study and this data is removed. However, after the above ﬁlters have been applied only one occurrence has a
genus rank. The remaining taxon ranks are SPECIES, SUBSPECIES and VARIETY. Finally, only data that has
taxonomic status "ACCEPTED" or "SYNONYM" is kept. This ﬁlter removes 1,112,938 occurrences from the
data set. The species synonyms, subspecies and varieties will subsequently need to be taxonomically aligned,
which will be addressed in section 1.3.
The pre-processing reduces the ﬁle size from 147GB to less than 4.6GB and the number of occurrence data
points from 142,271,907 to 127,924,654.
Below is an example of the syntax used in the AWK programming language (typed into the console as
1 line of code) which would read the tab delimited occurrence.txt ﬁle, remove rows where the taxon rank is
"GENUS" and print the 4 features decLat, decLong, species and taxonomic status as a semicolon delimited ﬁle:
awk ’BEGIN {FS="\t"; OFS = ";"}; { if ($200 !~ /GENUS/ )
print $133, $134, $204, $220 }’ path/occurrence.txt > path/outfile
1.3
Taxonomic alignment
The species occurrence data is collected for GBIF from many different sources and the recorded information is
not always correct. Also the standardisation of species naming is an on-going process. Because the goal of the
study is to ﬁnd the species richness, it is important to know the actual number of different species recorded at a
speciﬁc pH. If a species is counted more than once e.g., due to misspelling or a species being recorded under a
synonym, the species richness will be overestimated.
S135
==PAGE:171==
The taxonomic alignment was performed by producing a list of unique species names (binomial names
without author speciﬁcation for better matching) from the occurrence data and uploading it to the Taxonomic
Name Resolution Service (TNRS, http://tnrs.iplantcollaborative.org/TNRSapp.html.
The name processing settings were set to:
• Match accuracy: Don’t allow partial matching
• Match sensitivity: 0.7
• Sources: TPL; GCC; ILDIS; TROPICOS; USDA (NCBI excluded)
• Classiﬁcation: APGIII
• Processing mode: Perform name resolution
The only values that were changed from the default were the ﬁrst two. Partial matching (to a higher taxon) was
not allowed for the same reason that the GENUS taxon rank was removed; for this study it is critical that an
occurrence is attributed to a species and higher taxonomic ranks are not included. The match sensitivity deﬁnes
the level of fuzzy matching. The occurrence data from GBIF is generally of high quality, and matches found at
a lower sensitivity are not useful.
The result of the TNRS is used to harmonise the occurrence data species names. After inspecting the
results of the taxonomic alignment, only matches with an overall score ≥0.98 were kept, because matches
with lower scores were too different from the submitted name. This also indicates that the GBIF database has
already cleaned out most errors that the TNRS would normally be used for, such as misspelling and letter
permutation. SUBSPECIES and VARIETY taxon ranks are treated as synonyms by TNRS. Species names that
are "Accepted" or "Synonym" are kept and replaced with the accepted name, and occurrence data where the
TNRS reports "No opinion" or "Illegitimate" are removed from the data. After the taxonomic alignment there
remain 120,318,260 observations.
2
Assigning biomes and ecoregions to species occurrences
Because of the size of the species occurrence data and the complexity of the TEOW shapeﬁle it would be
impossible to perform spatial analysis in GIS software such as ArcGIS or QGIS. GIS methods from the
"rgdal" library in R v3.4.3 are used instead to overlay the occurrence data with the TEOW shapeﬁle and assign
ecoregions and biomes as a new feature in the text ﬁle containing the species occurrence data.
The ﬁle cannot be processed all at once and was therefore ﬁrst split into smaller ﬁles containing 200,000 lines
of data using the AWK programming language. Each sub-ﬁle was then processed using parallel programming
using the package "parallel" in R. The R program is run on a high-performance cluster, where each node has
between 20 and 32 cpus. This signiﬁcantly reduces the processing time from days to hours. 6,504,411 species
occurrences did not fall within the TEOW shapeﬁle polygons and were dropped from the data set. These
occurrences are probably located in the oceans and a fraction may be located in the 40 ecoregions deﬁned by
Olson et al. 2001 that are not included in the WWF TEOW data set.
After assigning biomes and ecoregions AWK was used to merge the subﬁles and subsequently split them
by biome, resulting in 15 new ﬁles; 14 biomes and one ﬁle with data where the biome could not be assigned.
S136
==PAGE:172==
This last ﬁle contains data where the coordinates are located outside the polygon fx in the oceans. It is possible
that some data along the borders of ecoregions fx coastlines are removed that could have been included in the
data set. It is however assumed that the amount of data removed in this way is insigniﬁcant compared to the
amount of data available for an entire ecoregion.
3
Soil pH
3.1
Data source
The International Soil Reference Information Centre (ISRIC) released an updated version of their SoilGrids
system in 2017. It provides global predictions for standard numeric soil properties including soil pH at 250m
resolution at 7 standard depths (0, 5, 15, 30, 60, 100 and 200 cm), and the data is publicly available at
files.isric.org/soilgrids/data/recent/.
SoilGrids 250m is calculated using Machine Learning with ca. 150,000 unique soil proﬁles used for training.
Areas that have extreme climatic conditions and/or have very restricted access are signiﬁcantly undersampled,
and the authors used expert-based pseudo-observations to ﬁll out these gaps. The soil proﬁles were combined
with a stack of 158 remote sensing-based soil covariates (surface attributes such as slope, vegetation, snow cover
etc) to produce the SoilGrids predictions. 10–fold repeated cross-validation gives an R2 value of 83.4% for soil
pH, meaning the model explains 83.4% of the variation in the data, which is a major improvement compared
to the SoilGrids1km system used in Crespo-Mendes et al. (2019) where only 50.5% of the variation in pH
was explained (Heuvelink et al., 2014). The RMSE (Root Mean Square Error) reported for SoilGrids250m
predictions of soil pH is 0.5 pH units.
3.2
Calculation of weighted average [H+]
For the present study it is assumed that the soil pH from 0 to 60cm depth is relevant in relation to plant root
exposure to acidifying substances. Therefore, SoilGrids250m data ﬁles for the soil pH were downloaded for the
entire world from www.SoilGrids.org for depths between 0 and 60 cm (excluding 100cm and 200cm)
in geographical WGS84 coordinates. The pH values are provided as 10 x the pH value in H2O solution. In
accordance with the recommendation of Ribeiro et al. (2017) the soil pH of each grid cell was calculated as a
weighted mean using numerical integration following the trapezoidal rule. The equation used is
µw =
1
b−a
Z b
a f(x)dx
(1)
=
1

N−1
∑

(xk+1 −xk)(f(xk)+ f(xk+1))
(2)
where a is the smallest depth (0cm), b is the largest depth (60cm), N is the number of depths, xk is the k-th
depth and f(xk) is the soil pH value at depth xk. Before computing the weighted average, the pH values are
converted to [H+] (concentration of H+ ions) using the equation
[H+] = 10−pH
(3)
S137
==PAGE:173==
All the above calculations were performed in R using the packages ’raster’ and ’parallel’. Each layer of the
soil pH data for the entire world contains about 1.6 billion grid cells and takes up approximately 2.5GB of
disk space as GeoTIFF ﬁles. The raster processing was parallelised and run on a high performance computing
cluster, so that the raster ﬁle could be processed in smaller chunks on many cores in parallel.
4
Assigning [H+] to occurrence data
The GBIF occurrence data is overlayed with the SoilGrids data to ﬁnd the [H+] at the location where each
occurrence data point is georeferenced. There is a mismatch between the coverage of the TEOW and SoilGrids
data, where TEOW includes Antarctica and SoilGrids does not. This affects only biome 11. The GBIF
occurrence data located outside the extent of the SoilGrids data is removed from the data set and 125 data
points are removed from biome 11.
Because the soil pH data is generated by a model, it has no "gaps" however, there are millions of grid
cells with "missing data" fx at the locations of lakes and rivers. Due to the inaccuracy in the GBIF data’s
georeferences, many of the occurrences are reported within SoilGrids grid cells that have NA pH values. It is
also possible that a fraction of these species occurrences are aquatic species. It is assumed that the average
of the neighbouring grid cells to a grid cell with an NA value will be a good approximation to the pH value
where the occurrence data was recorded. These values are added to the SoilGrids data using a function from
the R raster library called "focal", and the occurrence data that was not assigned a pH initially are assigned an
estimated pH.
For the original SoilGrids data there are 2,655,166 species occurrences that are not assigned a pH, which
is 2% of the species occurrence data. Applying the focal approach once results in assigning pH to 1,839,597
species occurrences, which is 69% of the data. Applying it again results in assigning an additional 436,947
occurrences, increasing the fraction assigned to 86%. The remaining data that is not assigned a pH constitutes
0.3% of the species data, and is deemed negligible and removed from the data set resulting in a new data set of
128,141,688 species occurrence data with pH values assigned. Write about how focal approach affects data for
biomes and ecoregions respectively.
5
Fit statistics
For nonlinear regression it is not trivial to evaluate how well a ﬁt represents data. There can be two reasons for
ﬁtting a curve; one is to describe a relationship between a dependent variable and one or more independent
variables by estimating parameters that describe some property of the system that is being analysed. When
the parameters don’t represent a speciﬁc property, the ﬁt is mainly a way to extrapolate values from the ﬁt
where there are no data points. The present study is mainly an example of the second case, where the estimated
ﬁtting parameters are used to calculate effect factors and not to advocate taht the data belongs to an underlying
distribution. For the present study the aim is to perform regression that sufﬁciently describes the data so that
effect factors can be calculated from the estimated parameters.
There is no easy way to evaluate the goodness-of-ﬁt for non-linear regression, and instead the ﬁts are
evaluated using a range of complementary statistical methods. Most of these methods are mainly useful when
one already knows what the value should be for a "good" ﬁt, which is not known for the present study. However,
S138
==PAGE:174==
when evaluated in combination, these statistics give a good indication of whether the ﬁt succeeds in describing
the underlying data. The ﬁt statistics that are being applied are presented below.
The nls function ("non-linear least squares) from the "stats" R library, which was used to perform the
parameter estimation for the non-linear ﬁts, supplies a few goodness-of-ﬁt measures in its output. The Residual
Sum of Squares (RSS, lower is better), the log Likelihood (higher is better) and the AIC (Akaike’s Information
Criterion, lower is better) have been included in the range of ﬁt statistics. The RSS is what is being minimised
by the nls function, and shouldn’t be used directly for evaluating the goodness-of-ﬁt.
The runs test is applied to investigate if a curve deviates systematically from the data - if there are fewer
"runs" than expected where consecutive data points all fall either below or above the ﬁtted curve. A low p-value
indicates that there is a problem with the ﬁt, but there is no deﬁnite cut-off point.
For the logistic ﬁt biomes 2, 4, 5, 6 and 13 have suspicious p-values ranging between <0.1 and 0.06. The
lognormal ﬁt fails the runs test with p-values <0.1 for all biomes, which strongly suggests that the lognormal
function probably doesn’t describe the relationship between soil [H+] and species richness very well.
The D’Agostino-Pearson test of normality of the residuals tests whether the residuals are normally dis-
tributed, which is an underlying assumption of regression. If there is a skewness in the residuals, the ﬁt will
violate this assumption. A low p-value indicates that there is an underlying issue. The logistic ﬁt performs
worse than the lognormal ﬁt in this test.
The standard evaluation method R2 evaluates linear regression, but it is possible to adjust it to be applicable
for non-linear regression (Kvalseth, 1985; Alexander et al., 2015) using the following equation
R2 = 1−∑(y−ˆy)2
∑(y−¯y)2
(4)
where y are the observed values, ˆy are the estimated values and ¯y i the average. This equation therefore
compares the size of the residuals between the model and a null model where all the predictions equal ¯y. The
adjusted R2, which takes into account the number of parameters in the model, can be calculated by dividing the
numerator with n−p (where n is the number of data points and n is the number of parameters) and dividing the
denominator with n−1. An R2
ad j close to 1 is a good indicator, and it should be noted that the values can be
negative, which would indicate a very poor ﬁt that probably has very few degrees of freedom.
The RMSE (root mean squared error) is also provided.
The Chi2 test is broadly applied as a goodness-of-ﬁt test, however for various reasons it performs poorly
for the present study. The statistic shows barely any difference between biomes or types of ﬁt and there is no
information to be found by applying this statistic in the present study.
6

determined that the lognormal distribution ﬁts the data best. Instead of ﬁtting a lognormal function to the PNOF
curve as a function of [H+], a 3 parameter normal (gaussian) function is ﬁtted to pH = −log10C), which yields
the same ﬁt. This makes the ﬁtting easier as the pH values do not span many orders of magnitude as does the
